The Estuary's Final Shrug

Chapter 1

CHAPTER ONE

Cobalt glass, stolen from a Saracen caravan and ground to powder, stained the lead of the new rose window the exact blue of a twilight sky. Brother Anselm’s thumb, cracked and chalky, smeared the pigment along a seam. He did not see a saint’s robe. He saw the color of a machine that had not yet been invented.

His shoulder struck a submerged log with a dull thud that he felt rather than heard. The impact spun him, and his head broke the surface. He gasped, and the air was almost as thick as the water, sweet with the scent of wet earth and blooming hawthorn. He saw, in a dizzying slice: the crumbling ochre face of a cliff, bearded with moss; the skeletal remains of a fishing weir, its blackened stakes like broken teeth; a flash of iridescent blue—a kingfisher, a living shard of stained glass—streaking away from the commotion.

Then the bank was upon him, a tangle of exposed roots like gnarled fingers. He grabbed one. It was slick, alive. It held.

For a long minute, he simply existed there, half in the chill embrace of the Aisling, half in the mud, coughing up river. His name was Cillian, and he was fourteen years old, and he was supposed to be dead. The thought arrived not with panic, but with a curious, detached clarity. The men who had thrown him from the bridge upstream, their faces hard as flint under their rusted nasal helms, had certainly thought so. A sacrifice to quiet the old gods, the one-eyed captain had grunted, as if ordering a barrel of ale. The river takes a tithe. It’s only balance.

Cillian hauled himself onto the bank, his body a symphony of new aches. He wore only a ragged linen shirt and breeches, now plastered to his skin like a second, colder hide. His boots were gone, taken by the river as payment. He sat on a cushion of moss, watching the water coil past. It did not look like a god. It looked like water, heavy with the spring melt from the mountains, carrying twigs and petals and the occasional gleaming bubble of swamp gas.

But he was alive. And being alive, unlike being dead, came with immediate and pressing requirements. The first was warmth. The second was direction. The third, a dull throb in his stomach reminded him, was food.

The forest that crowded the banks was not the tame, dappled woodland of song. This was the Weald. It was a maximalist’s delirium of life. Great oaks, each wider than a peasant’s hut, soared upward, their branches so dense they wove a second, leaf-choked sky. Between them, holly bushes grew in vicious, spiked thickets. Ferns uncurled in green fists. Moss covered every stone, every fallen log, in velvet blankets of emerald and umber. The air hummed with insects—fat, lazy bees and iridescent flies—and the birdsong was not pretty chirping but a raucous, overlapping liturgy of shrieks, whistles, and chattering alarms. It was too much. It was everything, all at once, shouting.

Cillian stood, his bare feet sinking into the cool, soft loam. He had no knife. No flint. No bread. He had the shirt on his back and the fact of his continued breath. It was, he supposed, a start.

He followed a boar trail, a narrow tunnel of trampled earth through the undergrowth, because it was the only thing that resembled a path. The light was a filtered, green-gold substance, thick as honey. He moved quietly, a habit born not of skill but of a lifetime spent trying to occupy less space. He was a reed-thin boy with hair the colour of damp straw and eyes that were neither blue nor grey but the shifting hue of the winter sky before snow. He looked like what he was: the orphaned by-blow of a minor cleric and a village weaver, useful only for running messages and drawing water until he’d been deemed useful for something else entirely.

The smell of woodsmoke found him before he saw the fire. It was a thin, acrid thread in the vegetal soup of the forest. Not the hearty peat-smoke of a village hearth, but something sharper. Resinous. He froze, then crept forward, parting a curtain of ivy with a trembling hand.

In a small clearing, a man was feeding a small, defiant blaze with sticks of dry pine. He was not a forester. He was not a hermit, at least not of the holy variety. He was a spectacle.

He wore a surcoat that had once been a deep, rich violet but was now faded to the colour of a week-old bruise, slashed over a hauberk of rust-speckled mail. Over that, he had draped a cloak of what looked like twenty different animal pelts—badger, fox, rabbit, and things with spotted fur Cillian couldn’t name—stitched together with crude, whip-like seams. His hair was a great, grey mane, shot through with stubborn streaks of black, tied back with a leather thong. His face was a topography of scars, wrinkles, and a magnificent, drooping moustache that flowed into a beard braided with what appeared to be tiny bird bones and brass rings.

But it was his hat that commanded attention. It was a felted thing, broad-brimmed and moth-eaten, from which sprouted an entire ossuary: the delicate skull of a weasel was pinned to the crown; a raven’s wing, still holding a few glossy black feathers, jutted from the side; a string of dried mushrooms, a squirrel’s tail, and several corroded pilgrim badges dangled from the brim. It was less a piece of clothing and more a small, mobile ecosystem.

The man was muttering to himself as he poked the fire. “No, no, you greedy bastard, not like that. You’ll choke on it. A fire’s like a woman, wants a gentle touch and good fuel.” His voice was a gravelly rumble, punctuated by phlegmy clears of his throat.

Cillian’s foot snapped a twig.

The man moved faster than his assemblage suggested possible. In one fluid motion, he rose, turned, and drew a weapon from beside his bedroll. It was not a sword. It was a long, heavy, brutally simple tool: a goedendag. A “good day” club. A stout oak shaft topped with a thick, iron-shod knob. He held it with the casual lethality of a man who had ended many days with it.

“Who’s there?” he boomed. “Come out from the green before I introduce your skull to my friend here. He’s feeling sociable.”

Cillian stepped into the clearing. He must have been a pitiful sight: dripping, shivering, barefoot, and empty-handed.

The man’s fierce expression didn’t soften, but it did shift into one of profound assessment. His eyes, a surprisingly clear and sharp grey, scanned Cillian from head to toe. “Hmph. River’s been at you, boy. And not in a friendly way. They throw you in, or did you jump?”

“Th-thrown,” Cillian managed, his teeth beginning to chatter.

“Who by?”

“Men. From the bridge keep. The… the Free Company of the Wolf’s Jaw.”

A snort, like a boar’s. “Free Company. Thieves and failed butchers who’ve bought a rusty mail shirt and a grandiose name. What was your crime? Steal a loaf? Look at a lord’s daughter?”

Cillian swallowed. “I was the tithe.”

The grey eyes narrowed. The man lowered the goedendag, leaning on it like a walking staff. “Tithe? To who? The bishop’s taxman’s got no use for drowned boys.”

“To the river. To quiet the old gods. For a good season.”

A long silence, broken only by the crackle of the fire and the shriek of a jay. The man’s face underwent a series of subtle transformations: contempt, then a flicker of understanding, then a deep, weary anger that seemed to settle in the lines around his mouth. “I see,” he said finally. “So they’ve gone back to that, have they? When prayers fail, try drowning. The logic of frightened sheep.” He jerked his head towards the fire. “Well, come on then. You’re blocking the light and you’re dripping on my kingdom. Sit. Before you fall over.”

Gratefully, Cillian stumbled to the opposite side of the fire and sank down. The heat was a physical blow, a glorious, painful thaw. The man rummaged in a large, oil-stained pack and produced a wooden bowl. He filled it from a small pot that had been nestled in the coals—a thin stew that smelled of onions, wild garlic, and something gamey. He thrust it at Cillian, along with a hunk of hard, dark bread.

“Eat. Slowly. Or you’ll puke it up and waste my good onions.”

Cillian ate. It was the most magnificent thing he had ever tasted. The broth was fire and salt and life itself. He forced himself to take small, slow bites of the bread, which tasted of rye and the smoke of the fire.

The man watched him, now seated on a stump, his goedendag across his knees. “Name’s Lothair,” he said, as if announcing a minor but important celestial event. “Some call me Lothair the Unlikely. Some call me other things, usually behind my back. You?”

“Cillian.”

“Cillian.” Lothair tested the name. “Means ‘little church.’ Ironic. The church’s men just tried to baptise you unto death.” He picked at a thread on his ridiculous surcoat. “So, Little Church. The river spat you out. The old gods, if they’re still listening, clearly have a poor appetite or a peculiar sense of humour. What do you intend to do now?”

Cillian stared into the stew. “I don’t know. I can’t go back.”

“A moment of stunning clarity. No, you cannot. To them, you are a fulfilled prophecy. A ghost would be inconvenient.” Lothair leaned forward, the bones on his hat clacking softly. “The world is full of paths, boy. You can wander into the Weald and become food for worms and wolves. You can try to find another village, where you’ll be a stranger with no name and work yourself to death on some strip of field. Or…” He paused, his eyes glinting in the firelight.

“Or?” Cillian whispered.

“Or you can follow me.”

“Where?”

“To the past,” Lothair said, as if it were obvious. “To the future. To the only place left where a river-tithe and a walking museum can find employ. To the Free City of Aevum.”

Cillian had heard of Aevum. It was a rumour, a story told by travelling peddlers and discharged soldiers. A city that was not a kingdom, that answered to no king, no emperor, no pope. A city of a hundred spires and a thousand forges, where guilds ruled and knowledge was a currency as solid as gold. A myth.

“It’s real?”

“As real as the mud on your feet. And it’s five days’ hard walk through this green hell. I am bound there, on a commission of… delicate acquisition.”

“You’re a thief?”

Lothair drew himself up, offended. “I am a retriever of antiquities. A liberator of lost patrimony. I find things that have been forgotten and deliver them to those with the wit to appreciate them. Currently, I am employed by the Guild of Horologists and Astrologers to retrieve a certain celestial map, last seen in the library of a monastery that recently and tragically burned down.” He smiled, revealing several gaps and one tooth of startling gold. “The fire was most fortuitous. And entirely not my fault.”

Cillian’s head swam. Monasteries. Celestial maps. Guilds. It was a vocabulary from another world. “Why would you want me?”

“Because, Little Church, you are now a creature of ill omen and good luck. A contradiction. The world has tried to erase you and failed. That makes you interesting. And,” he added, pragmatic again, “you can gather firewood, carry water, and watch my back while I sleep. I’m old enough to appreciate a second pair of eyes, even if they’re attached to a drowned rat.”

The stew sat warm in Cillian’s belly. The fire danced. The forest, for a moment, seemed less a crushing, hungry entity and more a vast, complicated tapestry. He had been offered a story, a role, however small, in a narrative far larger than being a tithe.

He looked at Lothair, at his bone-adorned hat, his fierce, intelligent eyes, his waiting, patient silence.

“Alright,” Cillian said.

“Alright?” Lothair echoed.

“I’ll follow you.”

Lothair nodded, as if a minor but satisfactory business transaction had been concluded. “Good. First lesson of the road: never trust a man who doesn’t appreciate a good hat.” He pointed his goedendag at Cillian’s feet. “And second lesson: we’re getting you some boots before you step on an adder and die for real. Sleep now. The Weald watches, but it watches everyone. We move at first light.”

And Cillian, wrapped in the smell of woodsmoke and strange pelts, with the immense, breathing darkness of the forest all around him and the memory of brown water in his mouth, slept.

Chapter 2

CHAPTER TWO

Dawn in the Weald was not a sunrise but a slow, green seepage. Light didn’t break; it percolated down through layers of leaves, emerging as a diffuse, aquatic glow. The birds began their riotous matins before the first hint of colour touched the sky.

Lothair was already moving, his camp broken down with an efficiency that belied his chaotic appearance. The fire was a circle of damp, black ash. His pack, a monstrous thing of leather and patched canvas, sat ready, bristling with rolled blankets, pots, and the mysterious shapes of tools. He tossed a bundle at Cillian, who was still blinking sleep from his eyes.

“Put those on. They’ll pinch, but they’re better than sepsis.”

They were boots. Old, cracked leather, scuffed to a soft grey, with thick soles studded with hobnails. They had belonged to someone with smaller feet than Lothair. A squire, perhaps, or a fallen comrade. Cillian pulled them on. They were stiff and cold, but they enclosed his feet in a firm, protective shell. The world felt different with boots on. More conquerable.

“Rule of the road,” Lothair intoned, hefting his pack onto his shoulders with a grunt. “You lead with silence. Your ears are your scouts. Your eyes are your guards. Your mouth is a liability until we’re clear of the deep wood. Understood?”

Cillian nodded.

“Good. Now, walk where I walk. Step where I step. The forest floor is a page, and every creature writes upon it. Learn to read.”

And so they moved. Lothair set a punishing, ground-eating pace, but it was not a blind rush. It was a deliberate, flowing navigation. He would pause, holding up a hand, to listen to the pattern of birdsong ahead. He would point to a patch of mud: the deep, cloven print of a stag, the delicate chain of a fox’s pads, the broad, splayed menace of a boar’s track. He showed Cillian how to avoid the slick, heart-shaped leaves of poison ivy, and which mushrooms, fat and white as loaves, could feed a man or kill him in screaming agony.

“This,” Lothair whispered once, crouching by a fallen elm, “is a badger’s sett. See the fresh spoil? He’s home. A grumpy neighbour, but an honest one. He’ll leave you be if you leave him be.” His fingers brushed a spiderweb beaded with dew, a necklace of diamonds strung between ferns. “And this is a cathedral. Respect it.”

Cillian’s mind, still numb from the shock of yesterday, began to wake. The overwhelming muchness of the forest started to resolve into details, into a language. He saw not just trees, but the way the ivy favoured the north side of the trunks. He heard not just noise, but the specific alarm call of a blackbird—a sharp pink-pink-pink—that meant ground disturbance. He was learning the grammar of the wild.

At midday, they stopped by a stream that chuckled over mossy stones. Lothair produced a wedge of hard cheese and a withered apple. They ate in silence, drinking the cold, clear water from their hands.

“The Free Company,” Cillian said finally, the words breaking the quiet like a thrown stone. “Will they come looking?”

Lothair took a bite of cheese, chewing thoughtfully. “For you? Unlikely. They performed their rite. They’ll have gotten drunk on their own piety and moved on to extorting the next village. For me?” He shrugged, the pelts on his cloak rustling. “Possibly. The monastery that burned was under the protection of their patron, a minor lord with more pride than sense. He might have hired them to track the ‘despoiler.’ But the Weald is a great forgetter of tracks. We are ghosts here.”

“What was in the map? The one you took.”

“Retrieved,” Lothair corrected. “It is a Mappa Coeli, drawn by a mad Arab astronomer who visited here two centuries ago. It shows the stars not as static jewels, but as… currents. Eddies in a great celestial river. The Horologists believe it may hold the key to predicting not just the movements of planets, but the confluence of events. The rise and fall of cities. The birth of monsters. The death of kings.” He said it without awe, as a carpenter might list the properties of different woods. “Nonsense, probably. But they pay in good silver.”

“You don’t believe it?”

“I believe in what I can hold, what I can trade, and what I can outrun.” Lothair’s grey eyes fixed on him. “What do you believe in, Little Church?”

Cillian looked at his reflection in the stream, fractured by the water. A pale, sharp face. A boy who should be at the bottom of a river. “I don’t know.”

“An excellent place to start. Better than being full of certainty. Certainty is a closed door.”

They moved on. The character of the forest began to change. The giant, primordial oaks gave way to more birch and ash, the undergrowth thinning. They were moving from the deep, sacred heart of the Weald towards its fringes. The air grew slightly warmer, less damp.

It was in this transitional zone that they found the shrine.

It was a small thing, almost swallowed by a thicket of hawthorn. A rough-hewn stone pillar, about waist-high, so weathered its carvings were mere ghosts of shapes. On its top sat offerings: a twist of dried berries, a few acorns, a small, crude clay figurine of a woman with wide hips and no face. At its base, fresh earth had been turned.

Lothair stopped, holding out an arm to bar Cillian’s way. His expression was unreadable. “Don’t touch.”

“What is it?”

“A memory. An old one. For the Matres, the Mother-Goddesses. This land is thick with them, if you know how to look.” He circled the shrine slowly, his eyes on the ground. “The offering is recent. Within a day. Not from a villager—they’d leave bread, milk. Berries and a figurine… that’s older magic. Deeper.”

A prickle walked up Cillian’s spine. The green light seemed to dim. The birdsong had stopped.

“Someone else is in the wood,” Lothair murmured. “Someone who remembers the old ways without the… theatrical drowning.” He bent closer to the turned earth. Then he froze.

Very slowly, he straightened up. In his hand, he held not a leaf or a stone, but a long, slender feather. It was a deep, iridescent blue-black, but its tip was stained with a rusty, unmistakable brown.

Blood.

“Not a bird’s,” Lothair said, his voice flat. “The shaft is too thick. This is a fletching. From an arrow.”

All the lessons of the morning coalesced into a single, cold point of understanding. They were not alone, and their company was not friendly. The silence of the birds was not reverence; it was warning.

“We move. Now. Not on the trail.” Lothair’s voice was low, urgent. He shoved the feather into his belt and pointed away from the shrine, into a denser stand of young pines. “Quick and quiet. Like the fox.”

Cillian’s heart hammered against his ribs. The boots that had felt so solid now seemed clumsy. He followed Lothair into the pines, the needles soft and silent underfoot. The Retriever moved with a new, predatory grace, his goedendag now held ready in one hand.

They had gone perhaps two hundred paces when Cillian saw it. A flicker of movement, a dappling of shadow that didn’t match the pattern of the trees. To their left, paralleling their course.

He grabbed Lothair’s cloak. Without a word, he pointed.

Lothair’s eyes followed. He gave a single, grim nod. He had seen it too.

“One,” he mouthed. “Scout.”

He changed direction, angling sharply right, forcing whoever was tracking them to adjust. The forest floor began to slope downward into a shallow ravine choked with brambles. It was terrible terrain, but it offered cover.

A whistle cut the air. High, short, like a bird, but too regular. An answer came from further ahead.

“Two,” Lothair muttered. “They’re herding us.”

Panic, cold and sharp, rose in Cillian’s throat. This was the forest’s true face. Not a teacher, but a theatre for violence.

Lothair stopped, pressing his back against the thick, scaly bark of a pine. He looked at Cillian, and for the first time, the boy saw not just fierceness or eccentricity, but a calculated, veteran’s resolve. “Listen to me, Little Church. They are not the Free Company. Their methods are… subtler. They want the map. They may want me. They have no use for you. If it goes badly, you run. You follow the sun west. You’ll find a charcoal-burner’s track. Follow it north. Understand?”

Cillian shook his head, a stubborn, terrified refusal.

“Understand?” Lothair hissed, his grip on the club tightening.

Before Cillian could answer, a man stepped onto the path ahead.

He was tall and lean, dressed in clothes the colour of forest decay—mottled greens and browns. He wore no mail, only a stiff leather jerkin. His face was long, pale, and utterly expressionless. In his hands he held a bow, not drawn, but an arrow nocked. Its fletching was blue-black.

“Lothair the Unlikely,” the man said. His voice was as dry as dead leaves. “You have something that belongs to my employer.”

“Everything I have, I’ve rightfully retrieved,” Lothair called back, his tone conversational, but his body was coiled like a spring. “You can tell your employer, whoever he may be, that his claim is noted and dismissed.”

“The Mappa Coeli,” the archer said, ignoring him. “Place it on the ground. Step away. The boy can go.”

“And me?”

“My employer would like to discuss your future career. In person.”

Lothair smiled his gap-toothed smile. “I’m retired from tutoring.” He shifted his weight, ever so slightly. “Cillian. The ravine. Now.”

The archer’s eyes flickered to Cillian. It was all the distraction Lothair needed.

With a roar that seemed to shake the pine needles, Lothair charged. He did not run at the archer, but at a slight angle, closing the distance in a staggering, zig-zag rush. The archer, coolly professional, drew and loosed in one fluid motion.

The arrow hissed through the space where Lothair’s chest had been a half-second before, thudding into a tree.

Cillian didn’t think. He ran. He plunged down the slope into the brambles, thorns tearing at his new shirt, his face. He heard another whistle, another answering call from his right. They were everywhere.

He scrambled, fell, rolled, and came to a stop behind a fallen, moss-covered log. He peered over it, his breath ragged in his throat.

Lothair had closed the gap. The goedendag was a blur of dark oak. The archer had dropped his bow and drawn a long, thin dagger. They circled, a deadly dance in the dappled light. Lothair was stronger, but the archer was faster. The dagger flickered out, drawing a red line across Lothair’s forearm. Lothair grunted and swung the club in a wide, brutal arc. The archer ducked under it, but the movement put him off balance.

From the trees to Cillian’s right, a second figure emerged. Another archer, this one drawing a bead on Lothair’s back.

No. The word formed in Cillian’s mind, clean and sharp as the thorn in his palm. He was not a tithe. He was not a ghost. He was a boy in borrowed boots, and this ridiculous, bone-clad man was the only thread connecting him to a world larger than a river’s bottom.

He did not shout. He did not throw a stone. He simply stood up.

The second archer saw the movement, his aim wavering for a fraction of a second. It was enough.

Lothair, sensing the shift, dropped low and swept the goedendag at the legs of the first archer. There was a sickening crack. The man cried out and fell. Lothair was on him in an instant, the iron knob coming down once, decisively. He didn’t wait to see the result. He spun, hurling the heavy club like a throwing axe at the second archer.

It was a desperate, wild throw. It missed. But it forced the man to duck behind a tree.

“Cillian! TO ME!” Lothair bellowed, snatching up the fallen bow and quiver.

Cillian scrambled up the slope, his heart in his mouth. Lothair grabbed him by the collar, shoving him forward. “Run! Don’t look back!”

They crashed through the forest, no longer stealthy, driven by pure survival. Shouts echoed behind them. An arrow zipped past, burying itself in an ash tree with a thwock.

They ran until the light began to fail and Cillian’s lungs felt like burning coals. Only when they had crossed another stream and climbed a steep, rocky ridge did Lothair finally stop, leaning against a boulder, gasping for air. Blood seeped from the cut on his arm, staining his patched sleeve.

He looked at Cillian, his chest heaving. Then he did a strange thing. He laughed. It was a rough, pained sound, but it was genuine.

“You stood up,” he wheezed. “Idiot boy. Magnificent, suicidal idiot.” He shook his head, the bones on his hat clattering. “They would have had me. A second’s distraction. That’s all it is, sometimes. The difference between a conversation and a corpse.”

Cillian couldn’t speak. He just nodded, his whole body trembling with spent adrenaline.

Lothair pushed himself upright. He examined the stolen bow, then tossed it into the ravine below. “Too identifiable. But they know we’re here now. The game has changed.” He looked at the darkening woods, his face grim. “No fire tonight. Cold camp. We walk through the dark. We’re closer to the edge than they think.”

He turned to Cillian, and his gaze was different. No longer assessing a burden, but acknowledging a presence. “You’re not just a pair of hands anymore, Little Church. You’re a accomplice. Welcome to the trade.”

He handed Cillian a strip of dried meat from his pack. “Eat. We have a long night ahead. And from now on, you keep a knife.” He passed him a small, sharp dagger in a leather sheath. “Try not to cut yourself. Or me.”

Cillian took the knife. It felt heavy. Real. A tool, not for sacrifice, but for survival. The forest around them was a vast, black mystery, full of teeth and arrows and ancient shrines. But he was in it now, a part of its story.

He followed Lothair into the gathering dark, the taste of river water finally gone, replaced by the taste of blood and cold night air.

Chapter 3

CHAPTER THREE

The world narrowed to the sound of Lothair’s breathing ahead of him and the feel of the knife hilt against his palm. They moved through a darkness so complete it was a physical substance, a black wool pressed against the eyes. Cillian learned to see with his feet, feeling for roots and stones, and with his ears, parsing the night sounds—the scuttle of a vole, the distant, mournful call of an owl, the sigh of wind in the highest branches. The maximalist riot of the day-forest was gone, replaced by a minimalist’s theatre of whispers and hints.

Lothair was a ghost in the gloom. He stopped often, listening for pursuit, but the only sounds were those of the forest itself. Their flight had been swift, their turn into the rocky ridge clever. The archers, whoever they served, had lost the scent.

Near midnight, they emerged from the tree line onto a high, windy down. The sky, unveiled by the canopy, was a breathtaking sprawl of stars—not the friendly, familiar constellations of shepherd’s tales, but a furious, icy dusting, a million sharp points of light in a velvet void. The Milky Way was a ragged scar of luminous milk across the dome. Cillian stopped, his head tilting back, breath caught in his throat. He had never seen so much sky.

“Aevum lies under that,” Lothair said quietly, pointing to a patch of horizon where the stars seemed to swim in a faint, smoky haze. “The forges burn day and night. They stain the very heavens.” He squinted at the stars, then down at the land below. “We’ve made good time. The Weald’s back is broken here. See?”

Cillian followed his gaze. Below them, the land rolled away in gentle, moon-washed hills. The dense, knotted woodland gave way to patches of managed forest, coppiced woods, and the dark rectangles of cultivated fields. The smell was different too—less of damp rot and more of turned earth and distant hearth-smoke. They had crossed an invisible border.

“Whose land is this?” Cillian asked, his voice small in the vastness.

“Nominally? The Baron of Harrow’s. In practice? It belongs to the sheep, the crows, and whatever freeholders are stubborn enough to hold a strip against tax and tide.” Lothair began picking his way down the slope. “We’ll find a byre or a shepherd’s hut at dawn. Get a few hours of true sleep. Then, the road to Aevum.”

The concept of a road felt as mythical as the city itself. Cillian’s world had been a village, a river, and the forest. The idea of a paved path connecting places across the land was an abstraction.

Dawn found them in a sheltered valley, where a swift, narrow stream had been dammed to create a millpond. The mill itself was a low, stone building with a great, motionless wheel. Beside it stood a small, wattle-and-daub hut, smoke curling lazily from a hole in its thatched roof. A dog barked once, half-heartedly, then fell silent.

“Millers are notoriously unsociable and famously avaricious,” Lothair murmured, eyeing the hut. “Perfect. Avarice can be negotiated with.” He straightened his ridiculous hat, brushed a bit of moss from his pelts, and strode towards the door as if arriving for a banquet.

The man who answered Lothair’s knock was short, broad, and flour-dusted from head to toe, giving him the appearance of a perturbed ghost. His eyes were small and shrewd.

“We’re closed. No grain till Lammas,” he grunted, beginning to shut the door.

Lothair’s foot was in it. “Good sir! We seek not your flour, but the warmth of your hearth and the wisdom of your local knowledge. For which,” he added, the gold tooth glinting, “we are prepared to offer recompense.” He produced a small silver coin from a pouch at his belt, holding it up so it caught the morning light.

The miller’s eyes locked onto the coin. His suspicion remained, but it was now alloyed with calculation. “Silver’s scarce. Who are you? Not law, not with that… hat.”

“Travelling scholars,” Lothair said smoothly. “Bound for Aevum. My apprentice and I were set upon by brigands in the Weald. We seek only a few hours’ rest and some directions.”

The miller’s gaze flicked to Cillian, taking in his torn shirt, the new boots, the wide, tired eyes. He sniffed. “Brigands. Aye. They’re thick as fleas this season. Two hours. And that silver buys you a bowl of porridge each. No more.”

“A prince among millers,” Lothair declared, sweeping inside.

The hut was a single room, smoky, warm, and cluttered with the tools of the miller’s trade. They sat on stools by the fire. The porridge, when it came, was thick, salty, and filled with bits of rough grain. It was ambrosia. As they ate, Lothair drew the man out.

“The road to Aevum. Is it clear?”

“Clear enough,” the miller said, watching them eat as if they might steal the bowls. “For now. The Baron’s men patrol it, collecting their tolls. But there’s talk. Strange talk.”

“Oh?”

“Aye. Riders. Not the Baron’s. Not any lord’s from these parts. Dark cloaks, good horses. Moving fast, asking questions. They came through here three days past. Asked after a man matching your… description.” He said the last word with a faint sneer.

Lothair’s spoon paused halfway to his mouth. “Did they now. And what did you tell them?”

“Told them I mind my mill and my grain. Strangers are bad for business.” The miller’s eyes went back to the empty space where the silver coin had been. “But others might be more helpful. The toll-keeper at Harrow’s Ford, for one. He’s got a nose for trouble and a palm that’s always itchy.”

Lothair nodded, finishing his porridge. “My thanks. The road, then, to Harrow’s Ford?”

“Follow the stream east ‘til it meets the Stoneway. Turn north. A day’s walk. You’ll smell the ford before you see it—the tannery’s there.”

They slept for a few precious hours in the mill’s loft, buried in sweet-smelling hay. The deep, dreamless sleep of utter exhaustion. When Lothair shook him awake, the sun was high.

The Stoneway was not what Cillian had imagined. It was not a grand, paved Roman road, but a wide, muddy track, its surface a mosaic of ancient, worn flagstones and more recent potholes. But it was unmistakably a road. It cut through the landscape with purpose, ditched on either side, bearing the scars of countless cart wheels and hoofprints. It felt like the spine of the world.

They joined the flow of traffic. A carter with a load of wool, his oxen plodding and patient. A family of

Chapter 4

CHAPTER FOUR

The second thing the boy learned was the weight of stone. It was not the river’s fluid, swallowing weight, but a dense and patient press, a silence that settled into the marrow. The Stoneway, under the afternoon sun, was a slab of bleached bone. Each flagstone, polished by centuries of passage, held the day’s heat and threw it back at them in a shimmering veil. The dust they kicked up was a fine, white powder that tasted of chalk and old journeys. It coated the tongue, gritted between the teeth. Cillian walked in Lothair’s shadow, his body a single, throbbing note of exhaustion, but his eyes were open.

The traffic was a slow, discordant river of its own. The carter with his wool had fallen behind, but others took his place. A tinker with a rattling tower of pots, his face seamed like old leather. Two monks in brown robes, their feet bare and filthy, eyes fixed on some inward horizon. A woman driving a single, bony cow. They flowed around Cillian and Lothair without comment, a current of muted purpose. No one met their eyes. On the Stoneway, a gaze was a currency, and to spend it was to invite a transaction.

“Watch the feet,” Lothair said, his voice low, not turning his head. “Not the faces. The feet tell you everything. The merchant with the light step is successful, or running. The soldier drags his heels when he’s weary of it. The fearful man steps like he’s on ice.”

Cillian looked down. A pair of scuffed boots, toes capped in iron, trudged past. A set of bare, splayed feet, calloused as roots. A child’s quick, skipping steps, suddenly corrected by a sharp pull from a mother’s hand. It was a language he had never considered. He tried to listen.

“The road is a ledger,” Lothair continued. They had paused by a wayside cross, its wood silvered and split. “It records every debt. Every flight. Every return. To walk it is to add your line. Make yours a quiet one.”

They walked. The sun began its long, sloping fall, stretching their shadows into gaunt, striding giants ahead of them. The land here was more open, the dense river-valley forest giving way to rolling hills patched with gorse and heather. The sky was a vast, bowl of washed-out blue. Cillian felt exposed, a beetle on a tabletop. The comfort of the trees was a memory as distant as the taste of the river.

As dusk bled into the horizon, a settlement emerged from the haze. Not a town. A clutch of low, sod-roofed huts huddled around a wider, mud-churned section of the road, where the stream they had followed met a broader, slower river. The smell hit them first—a thick, pungent, and profoundly wrong sweetness, undercut with a sharp, chemical tang that clawed at the back of the throat. The tannery. The miller had not lied. You could smell Harrow’s Ford long before you saw it.

The ford itself was a shallow, stony crossing, marked by a leaning post with a faded emblem. On the far bank, a squat stone building squatted like a toad, a single window glowing with sullen orange light. The toll-house.

“Stay behind me. Say nothing,” Lothair murmured, his hand drifting to the worn hilt of his seax. “Let the ledger read: two tired men, nothing more.”

They splashed through the cool water, the stones slick underfoot. As they reached the other side, the door of the toll-house creaked open, spilling a rectangle of smoky light onto the mud. A man filled the doorway. He was broad, not with muscle but with a solid, settled fat, the kind that comes from taking a little from everyone. A leather apron, stained dark with things Cillian didn’t want to name, covered his front. His face was round, his eyes small and bright as a pig’s.

“Evening, travellers,” the man said. His voice was oddly high, a chirp from the great barrel of his chest. “A penny for the ford. A penny for the Baron’s peace.” He held out a thick, pink palm.

Lothair fished in his purse, produced a copper coin. He placed it in the palm. The man’s fingers closed, but did not withdraw.

“Two of you,” the toll-keeper chirped. “That’s two pennies. Baron’s peace is priced per head.”

Lothair was still. “The custom is a penny for the party. Since your father’s time.”

“Customs change. Like the weather. Like the price of salt. Two pennies.” The small eyes flicked over Lothair’s worn gear, lingered on Cillian’s too-thin face, his borrowed clothes. “Or you can swim back. The river’s free.”

A silence stretched, filled with the gurgle of the ford and the distant, rhythmic thump from the tannery vats. Lothair’s shoulders relaxed, a fraction. It was the most dangerous thing Cillian had seen him do. He added another copper to the outstretched hand.

The fingers snapped shut. “Wise. The Baron values wise men.” The toll-keeper stepped back, but his gaze was sharp, proprietary. “Seeking lodging? The ‘Ford’s Rest’ yonder.” He jerked a thumb toward the largest of the sod huts, from which a faint noise of voices and a smell of burnt stew emanated. “Tell them Odric sent you. They’ll treat you fair.”

“Our thanks,” Lothair said, the words empty as a discarded shell.

They turned toward the hut. Cillian felt the man’s eyes on their backs, a physical pressure between the shoulder blades. He remembered the miller’s words. A nose for trouble and a palm that’s always itchy.

The ‘Ford’s Rest’ was a single room, low-beamed and thick with smoke and the sour reek of ale. A peat fire smoldered in a central hearth. Men—carters, tanners, a few with the hard, vacant look of mercenaries between engagements—hunched on benches, speaking in low murmurs. The air tasted of damp wool and resignation.

Lothair secured a corner of packed-earth floor for the price of a single copper, and two bowls of a greasy stew that was mostly turnip. They ate in silence, their backs to the wall. Cillian watched the room. He saw the way a man’s eyes would dart to the door each time it opened. The way conversations died when a newcomer entered, then resumed in a different, lower key. This was not a place of rest. It was a place of pause, of held breath.

Later, in the dark, curled on the hard ground with his cloak pulled tight, Cillian listened to the sounds of the sleeping room: the snores, the mutters, the scratch of vermin in the thatch. He was hovering on the edge of a thin, troubled sleep when a new sound inserted itself—a soft, deliberate scuff of a boot near their corner.

He froze, his breath held. Through slitted eyes, he saw a shape crouched beside Lothair’s sleeping form. Not Lothair. The fire’s embers cast just enough glow to outline the toll-keeper’s round head, the gleam of one small eye. Odric’s fat hand was moving, slow as a hunting spider, toward the leather pack Lothair used as a pillow.

Cillian’s heart hammered against his ribs. He did not move. He did not breathe.

The hand was an inch from the pack when Lothair’s own shot out from beneath his cloak. There was no grab, no struggle. Simply a hard, precise clamp of fingers around Odric’s wrist. The sound of bone grinding against bone was terribly loud in the dark.

Odric let out a choked squeak.

“The Baron’s peace is expensive tonight,” Lothair whispered. His voice was calm, conversational, the voice of a man discussing the weather. “First two pennies. Now a thief’s fee. What shall we set that at, Odric? A finger? An eye?”

The toll-keeper was trembling. “A mistake… I thought you were another… I was checking—”

“You were checking the weight of my silver.” Lothair’s grip tightened. Odric whimpered. “Listen to me, you greedy grub. The men in dark cloaks on good horses. They will come here. They will ask you the same questions. And you will tell them you saw no one. You will tell them the miller was mistaken. You will tell them the road was empty. Because if I hear a whisper, a single, chirping little whisper, that says otherwise, I will not come for your fee. I will come for your tongue. And I will take it, not with a knife, but with my hands. Do you understand the ledger now?”

A frantic, jerking nod.

Lothair released him. Odric scrambled back, cradling his wrist, and vanished into the deeper shadows. The room’s sleep-sounds continued, undisturbed, a chorus of oblivious breath.

Cillian let out his own breath, a long, shaky stream. He felt a new kind of cold, one that had nothing to do with the earth beneath him.

“Sleep,” Lothair murmured, his voice already sliding back toward drowsiness. “The first lesson is always the same: the world is full of hungry things. You must decide, early, what kind of hunger you will feed.”

The morning was grey and drizzling, the tannery stench dampened to a wet, wool-like foulness. They left before first light, slipping from the ‘Ford’s Rest’ like ghosts. Odric was nowhere to be seen. The ford was unattended.

They walked north on the Stoneway, the drizzle beading on their wool cloaks. The incident of the night hung between them, unspoken. It was a new thread in the weave of their journey, dark and strong.

After several hours of silent travel, Lothair stopped where the road curved around a rocky outcrop. Below them, in a shallow valley, a small, fast-flowing creek crossed under the Stoneway via a narrow, single-arch bridge of ancient, mossy stone.

“We leave the road here,” Lothair said.

“Why?”

“Because Odric will talk. Fear is a dam. It holds for a time, but pressure always finds a weakness. The riders will check the ford. They will check the road. We will not be on it.”

He led them down the slope, through bracken that soaked their leggings, to the bank of the creek. “This is the Fiddlebeck. It leads into deeper woods, then into the high moors. A harder path. A quieter one.”

For three days, they followed the beck. It was a different world. The Stoneway’s open exposure was replaced by a close, green gloom. The trees here were older, their trunks thick with moss, their branches forming a tangled roof that turned the drizzle into a perpetual, dripping twilight. The path was not a path at all, but a suggestion along the rocky bank, requiring constant attention. Slippery stones. Hidden holes. Fallen trunks bearded with fungus.

This was Cillian’s training ground. Lothair’s lessons became practical, immediate, and merciless.

“Your footfall is a herd of cattle,” Lothair said, as Cillian sent a cascade of pebbles clattering into the water. “Walk on the outside of your foot. Roll your weight. Feel the ground before you trust it.”

He showed him how to read the moss on the trees for north. How to find crawfish under flat stones for food. How to sharpen the end of a green stick and fish for frogs in the muddy eddies. It was a knowledge of small, sure things. A grammar of survival.

The third evening, as they huddled under a rock overhang eating a paste of pounded acorns and a single, stringy frog, Lothair fixed him with a look.

“You have questions. The ones that are chewing at you. Ask one.”

Cillian stared into the tiny, smokeless fire of dry twigs Lothair had coaxed to life. The taste of the acorn paste was bitter, earthy. He thought of the river, the taste of peat and battles. He thought of the toll-keeper’s squeal.

“The riders,” Cillian said. “Who are they?”

Lothair poked the fire with a stick. “Men who serve a different hunger. They are the Hounds of the Interim Council.”

“The what?”

“When an Emperor dies without a clear heir, the machinery of empire does not stop. It is tended by an Interim Council of Archons. They are supposed to keep the wheels turning, to ensure a smooth succession.” He gave a short, bitter bark that was not a laugh. “The old Emperor has been dead for a year. The succession is not smooth. It is a knife-fight in a dark room. And the Archons… some of them have grown to like the taste of power. They would prefer the room stay dark.”

“And you?” Cillian asked, the boldness surprising him. “Which hunger do you serve?”

Lothair was silent for a long time. The only sound was the drip-drip from the moss and the chuckle of the Fiddlebeck. “I served an idea,” he said finally, his voice softer than Cillian had ever heard it. “The idea that the room should not be dark. That the line should not break. I was a… custodian of that idea. Now, I am just a man trying to deliver a package to the right door, before the wrong hands tear it open.”

“What’s in the package?”

A ghost of a smile touched Lothair’s lips. “That is not your question to ask. Yet.” He tossed the stick into the fire. “Your turn to watch. First shift. Listen for the water. If it stops, something is blocking it. Wake me.”

The Fiddlebeck led them up and out of the woods onto the high moors. The world opened up again, but this was a desolate, sweeping openness. The sky was a vast, bruised canopy of rushing grey cloud. The wind was a constant, keening presence, shearing the heat from their bodies, whipping the heather into silver-green waves. In the distance, great tors of granite thrust from the earth like the broken bones of giants.

It was here, on the afternoon of the fourth day, that they found the shepherd.

Or rather, he found them. They were picking their way along a sheep track, the wind tearing at their words, when a figure rose from behind a peat hag. He was a small, wiry man, wrapped in a cloak of uncured sheepskin that made him look like a moving piece of the landscape. In his hands was a long, crooked staff. His face was narrow, sharp, and as weathered as the granite.

“Lost,” the shepherd stated. It wasn’t a question.

“Passing through,” Lothair called back over the wind.

“A poor place for it. The moor eats the lost.” The shepherd’s eyes, a pale, watery blue, scanned them. They lingered on Cillian with an unsettling intensity. “The boy has the river still on him.”

A chill that had nothing to do with the wind went through Cillian. Lothair’s hand moved subtly toward his seax.

“What do you mean?” Lothair asked, his voice flat.

“I mean the peat preserves things. Stories. Souls. I taste the old water in him. The cold, brown deeps.” The shepherd took a step closer. He didn’t smell of sheep, but of peat smoke and something herbal, sharp. “You seek the old roads. The roads under the roads.”

“We seek Aevum,” Lothair said.

“Aevum is a word. A direction. I speak of a path.” The shepherd pointed his staff not north, but northeast, toward a particularly jagged line of tors. “There. A valley the maps forget. A circle of standing stones. A door, of a sort. It is a quicker path for those the river has touched. If you dare it.”

“What is the price for this guidance?” Lothair’s voice was pure suspicion.

“No price. Only an observation. The dark-cloaked riders passed along the Stoneway two days ago. They had hounds with them. Not the four-legged kind. The two-legged, sniffing kind. They are casting a wide net.” The shepherd’s pale eyes held Lothair’s. “The moor can hide you. Or it can swallow you. The choice of path is yours, river-man.”

He turned then, and with a few strides, seemed to melt back into the folds of the moor, becoming just another shadow in the heather.

“A madman,” Cillian said, his heart pounding.

“Perhaps,” Lothair murmured, staring at the line of tors. “Or perhaps a piece of the ledger we cannot read. The moor is an old place. It remembers older ways.” He looked at Cillian, truly looked at him, as if seeing the taste of the river on him for the first time. “He said a door.”

“It could be a trap.”

“Everything is a trap. Or a path. The difference is often only clear in the walking.” Lothair squared his shoulders. “We go to the stones.”

The valley was not on any map because it seemed barely part of the world. They found it as the last grey light of day was bleeding from the sky. A crack in the moor, hidden until you stood on its lip. A steep descent into a bowl where the wind’s scream became a muted sigh. And in the center, a circle of seven standing stones.

They were not the towering, majestic monoliths of legend. They were low, squat, and covered in a thick, velvety pelt of moss and orange lichen. They leaned inward, as if sharing a ancient, silent secret. The air in the valley was still, warm, and carried a faint, ozone scent, like the moment after a lightning strike.

In the very center of the circle was a flat, altar-like stone. And on it, sitting cross-legged as if waiting for them, was a woman.

She was perhaps Lothair’s age, but where his was a weariness of muscle and road, hers was a sharp, watchful fatigue. Her hair, black streaked with iron grey, was bound in a single, severe braid. She wore practical, travel-stained leathers, but over them was a cloak of a strange, iridescent fabric that seemed to drink the fading light. A long, narrow blade lay across her knees. Her eyes were the colour of flint.

“Lothair of the Silent Steps,” she said. Her voice was low, rasping, like stone grinding on stone. “You are late. And you have brought the current with you.” Her flint-eyes flicked to Cillian. “The river’s orphan.”

Lothair had gone very still. A series of emotions Cillian could not name flashed across his face—shock, wariness, a faint, reluctant hope. “Elara. I thought you were at the White City.”

“The White City is a hive of whispers and drawn knives. I prefer the open venom of the moor.” She stood in one fluid motion. She was tall. “The Hounds are a day behind you. They have a Sensitive with them. He’s weak, but he’s caught the scent of your… package.” She said the word with the same inflection Lothair used. “You cannot outrun a scent on the wind.”

“Then we stand,” Lothair said, his hand on his seax.

“We are three. They are eight. And a Sensitive, however weak, turns a fight into a slaughter. He will find the fear in your boy’s heart and twist it until he screams your position to the sky.” Elara’s gaze was pitiless. “We do not stand. We unmake the scent.”

She walked to the edge of the stone circle, to a patch of damp, dark earth. From a pouch at her belt, she took a handful of small, wrinkled, grey-green leaves. Crushing them in her fist, she let the fragments fall onto the soil. Then she drew a silver needle from her braid, pricked her thumb, and let three drops of dark blood fall amidst the leaves.

“Come here, boy,” she commanded.

Cillian looked at Lothair. Lothair, after a heartbeat’s hesitation, nodded.

Cillian approached. The air around the crushed leaves seemed to shimmer, though the light was nearly gone.

“Your hand,” Elara said. He extended it. She took it, her grip dry and strong as root. The silver needle flashed. A sharp sting on his thumb. She squeezed a single, fat drop of his blood onto the mixture. “The river’s claim,” she whispered, not to him, but to the earth.

She began to chant. The words were guttural, old, shapes of sound that seemed to warp the quiet air of the valley. The ozone smell intensified. The moss on the nearest standing stone seemed to pulse with a faint, greenish light.

As she chanted, she gestured for Lothair. He came, and without being asked, offered his thumb. His blood joined theirs.

Elara knelt, gathered the bloody, leafy earth into her palm, and breathed upon it. Then she stood, walked to the center of the circle, and scattered the mixture to the four winds.

A silence fell, deeper than before. The ozone scent vanished.

“It is done,” Elara said, wiping her hand on her cloak. “To a Sensitive, you will now smell of nothing. Of stone. Of old rain. You are a gap in the world’s scent. For a time.”

“A blood-price,” Lothair said, his voice tight.

“All prices are blood-prices,” Elara replied. “The question is whose. Tonight, it was ours. A few drops to save the quarts they would spill.” She sheathed her blade. “We have until dawn before the charm frays. We move. There is a place.”

They moved through the night, Elara leading with an unerring certainty that made Lothair’s woodcraft seem like a child’s game. She chose paths that were not paths, over ground that left no trace. They did not speak. The shared ritual in the stone circle hung between them, a new and potent thread. They were no longer a man and a boy. They were three. A number with strength.

Just before dawn, they came to a secluded gorge where a waterfall cascaded into a deep, black pool. Behind the curtain of water was a cave, hidden from the world.

“The Rider’s Sorrow,” Elara said, as they crouched in the damp, echoing space behind the roaring water. “A place to breathe.”

They shared a meal of waybread from Elara’s pack and cold water from the pool. It was the first real respite they’d had since the miller’s loft. The exhaustion hit Cillian like a falling wall. But beneath it was a new, fragile thing. A sense of not being alone against the hungry dark.

As the grey light of morning began to glow through the waterfall, Lothair spoke to Elara in low tones. Cillian, fighting sleep, caught fragments.

“…the package is intact…” “…the White City is impossible now. The Archons have sealed it…” “…then we go to the Witan. The old law still holds in the fens…” “…A dangerous gamble. The Fen-Queen’s loyalty is to herself…”

Cillian’s eyes closed. The roar of the water became the roar of the river. But it was a distant thing. Here, behind the water, with the scent of stone and old rain on him, he felt, for the first time, hidden. Not just from men, but from the world itself.

He slept.

He was woken by a hand on his shoulder. It was Lothair. The light beyond the waterfall was the gold of late afternoon. He had slept the day away.

“Time,” Lothair said. His face was grim, but there was a determined set to his jaw. Elara was already at the cave mouth, a silhouette against the brilliant, watery light.

“The charm has faded,” she said, without turning. “The void we made has filled. They will be casting again. But we have a start. And a destination.”

“The fens,” Lothair said to Cillian. “We go to the last court that might still hold to the old oaths. To the Witan of the Hundred Isles.”

They slipped out from behind the waterfall, into the cool, spray-filled air. The pool below was a mirror, perfectly reflecting the sky. For a moment, Cillian saw them there—three figures, blurred by the mist, standing on the edge of the known world.

Elara took point, moving upstream along the gorge. Lothair gestured for Cillian to follow. As he took his first step, a sound cut through the constant roar of the falls. Distant, but clear on the wind that swirled down the gorge.

A horn. A long, mournful, hunting note.

It was not behind them. It was ahead.

Elara froze. Lothair’s seax was in his hand, the sound a whisper of finality.

The horn sounded again, closer now. And from the rim of the gorge above them, a voice, amplified by the stone walls, rang out. It was a voice of polished malice, cold and clear as ice.

“Lothair! We have your scent again! And we have the high ground. Send the boy out. Send him out, and you and your traitorous friend may yet walk away. It is the only line left in your ledger.”

Cillian looked up. Silhouetted against the afternoon sky, along the lip of the gorge, were seven figures. Dark cloaks. Good horses. And in the center, a smaller figure on foot, his hands extended, palms downward, as if feeling the heat of their lives rising from the stones below.

Chapter 5

CHAPTER FIVE

The second thing Cillian knew was the taste of stone. It was a dry, dusty, and patient taste, the taste of a mountain holding its breath. It coated his tongue as he stood, frozen, under the waterfall’s dying roar. The horn’s note had dissolved into the gorge, but its echo lived in the marrow of his bones. It was not a sound of pursuit. It was a sound of conclusion. The world had drawn a circle around them, and the circle was closing.

Elara did not look back. Her stillness was not fear; it was calculation, the quiet of a hawk judging the wind. Lothair’s seax, that dull sliver of finality, did not waver. His eyes were on the silhouettes above, counting, measuring the angles of the rock.

“The high ground,” Lothair murmured, the words barely a breath. “But the river has the last word in this place.”

The smaller figure on the rim—the one with his palms to the stone—spoke again. His voice was a wire drawn tight across the chasm. “You have until the count of one hundred. A courtesy, for old times. Then we come down. And we will not be gentle with the stone.”

“Athelstan,” Lothair said, the name a curse and a recognition. “He feels for the hollows we leave in the air. The charm’s ghost.”

“He’s a dowser,” Elara said, finally turning. Her face was all hard angles in the spray-light. “But he dowses for life. For the warmth of a soul’s passage. He can’t see us, not clearly. Not yet. He feels the impression we left, like a warm seat. The waterfall’s breath confuses it. But it won’t for long.”

“One hundred,” Cillian said, the words ash in his mouth.

“Ninety now,” Lothair corrected. He sheathed his seax. The gesture was not surrender. It was the acceptance of a new set of rules. “We do not go out. We go down.”

He pointed not to the gorge walls, but to the pool at their feet. The mirror that held the sky. It was deep, a bowl carved by a millennium of falling water, its edges lost in froth and shadow.

“The river speaks,” Lothair said. “It says the only way out is through.”

Elara’s eyes met his, and a flicker of understanding passed between them—a language older than words. She gave a single, sharp nod. “The underground course. The stories say it empties into the fens.”

“Stories are all we have left,” Lothair said. He began stripping his pack, keeping only a waxed leather pouch and his seax. “The boy will need a guide rope. The current under the mountain is… persuasive.”

Cillian’s blood turned to the cold peat-taste of the river from his memory. The indifferent, muscular god. “We drown.”

“We might,” Lothair agreed, without looking at him. “Or we might find the Witan. Athelstan will not follow. His art is of the earth and the air. Running water scours it clean. He’ll lose the scent for good. But he knows the fens are our only haven. He will ride for the nearest crossing and be waiting. This is not an escape. It is a choice of battlegrounds.”

“Sixty,” Elara said, her voice detached, as if counting heartbeats. She was already tying a length of braided gut line around her waist, passing the other end to Lothair. “The pool has an undercut. A lip. The current will pull you down and through. Do not fight it. Let it take you. The rope is for the darkness, so we do not become lost from each other in the belly of the world. Kick when you feel the pull lessen. Up is the only direction that matters then.”

Lothair tied the rope’s other end around his own waist, leaving a long, slack span between them. He motioned Cillian to the middle. “Tie it. Tight. You are the link in the chain. If you let go, we are all undone.”

Cillian’s fingers were numb, but he fumbled the knot, pulling it until it bit into his flesh. The rope was a dead thing, a dried intestine, but it felt like the only living tether he had left.

“Thirty,” Elara said. She stepped to the pool’s edge. The reflected sky shattered at her feet. She did not hesitate. She inhaled once, deeply, a sound like a blade being drawn, and stepped off the ledge into the mirror.

She did not splash. She was consumed. The water folded over her head without a ripple, as if she had been translated into another element. The rope paid out, snaking after her into the dark.

“Fifteen,” Lothair said. He put a heavy hand on Cillian’s shoulder. His grip was iron, but his eyes were the strange, soft grey of a winter dawn. “The river does not care. But I do. Now, breathe.”

He pushed.

The world became a roaring, black, and absolute thing. The cold was a fist around his heart. The mirror-surface shattered above him, a fleeting kaleidoscope of gold and green, then was gone. The waterfall’s thunder became a muted, pervasive shudder in the stone. He was tumbling, the rope a burning line around his middle. He remembered Elara’s words. Do not fight it.

He went limp.

The current under the mountain was not a god. It was a heartbeat. A vast, slow, pulsing thrum that took him into its rhythm. It pulled him down along the smooth-rock lip, then sideways into a throat of stone. The pressure in his ears was immense. Darkness was not an absence here; it was a substance, thick and velvety and total. He saw nothing. He felt only the tug of the rope ahead, the drag of the rope behind, and the insistent, muscular pull of the water carrying him forward into the mountain’s gut.

Time dissolved. It became the count of heartbeats, the burn in his lungs. A panic, white and sharp, began to claw at the edges of his surrender. He needed air. The need was an animal, gnawing at his reason. He kicked, a frantic, instinctive rebellion.

A hand found his ankle in the dark. A firm, denying grip. Lothair. The message was clear. Not yet.

He forced his limbs to stillness. The animal screamed. His vision sparked with false stars in the black. Just as the scream was about to become a final, drowning gasp, the heartbeat of the current changed. The pull lessened. The water seemed to widen, to grow lazy.

The hand on his ankle gave a sharp, urgent yank. Now.

Cillian kicked upward with all the strength of his terror. He drove his feet down, clawed at the water above his head. His lungs were fire. His head struck stone—a glancing, terrifying blow—and he veered, disoriented. Then his face broke into a void.

He gasped, and the air was cold and smelled of mushrooms and deep, wet clay. It was the sweetest taste of his life. He tread water, choking, blinking into the black. A hand grabbed the back of his tunic.

“Here,” Lothair’s voice echoed, hollow and vast. “To the side.”

He was hauled onto a shelf of gritty stone. He lay on his back, heaving, water pouring from his mouth and nose. A moment later, he heard Elara’s measured breathing nearby, the sound of her wringing out her hair.

A light flared—a small, shielded lantern Lothair had kept in his waxed pouch. The flame was a blasphemy in that primordial dark, but Cillian could have wept for it. They were in a cavern, a bubble in the bedrock. The pool they had emerged from lay like a pool of ink in the floor. The ceiling was a low, dripping tapestry of stone teeth. The air was still and profoundly silent, save for the drip of water and the ragged sound of their breathing.

Elara checked the rope, then untied it. “The river goes on,” she said, her voice a whisper that the cavern swallowed. “But this is a pocket. A lung. We can follow the bank. The air moves that way.” She pointed to a low, black arch at the cavern’s far end. A faint, cool breath issued from it.

Lothair held the lantern high. The light glinted in Elara’s eyes, and Cillian saw something there he had not seen before: a raw, unguarded fatigue. “Athelstan will be riding hard for the Sallow Crossings,” she said. “We have bought time, not freedom.”

“Time is the only currency that matters now,” Lothair said. He looked at Cillian. “Can you walk?”

Cillian pushed himself up. His legs trembled, but they held. He nodded.

“Then we walk into the past,” Lothair said, turning toward the black arch.

They walked for hours in a darkness that was older than kings. The lantern light painted a small, bobbing world around them: walls of smooth, water-sculpted stone, floors of sand and pebbles, ceilings that sometimes soared into invisible heights and sometimes forced them to crawl. The air moved, that constant, cool exhalation from somewhere ahead, carrying the scent of decay and growing things. It was the breath of the fens.

They did not speak. Speech was too small a thing for that place. Their world was reduced to the scrape of boot on stone, the drip of water, the rhythm of their own hearts. Cillian’s mind, stripped of sight and the immediate panic of pursuit, began to turn inward. The cold water had washed something clean, not away, but to the surface.

He saw the boy in the river. Not as a memory, but as a reflection in the dark glass of the present. That boy had been empty, a vessel for brown water and ancient rot. Who was he now? A rope between two strangers. A quarry. A question mark. He had no past that he could hold, only the future that others saw for him—a prize, a key, a burden.

“Lothair,” he said, his voice startlingly loud in the tunnel. It was the first word any of them had spoken since the cavern.

Lothair stopped. The lantern light swung back, painting his face in stark relief. He said nothing, waiting.

“The Witan of the Hundred Isles,” Cillian said. “What will they see when they look at me?”

Lothair was silent for a long moment. The darkness pressed in around the pool of light. “They will see a thread,” he said finally. “A thread that connects a murder in the dark to a throne that sits empty. They will see a story they thought was finished. Whether they welcome that story or burn it is the gamble we take.”

“And you?” Cillian asked. “What do you see?”

Elara had turned, a shadow at the edge of the light. She was listening.

Lothair’s gaze was unflinching. “I saw a boy who did not struggle in the river. That is a rare thing. Most men fight the current until it breaks them. You let it carry you. That is either profound wisdom or the emptiness of one who has nothing left to lose.” He paused. “Today, in the pool, you fought. For a second. Then you stopped. That is the difference. That is what I see. A boy learning what to fight for.”

He turned and continued walking. The conversation was over.

But it had opened a space. As they navigated a narrow ledge over a subterranean stream whose voice was a deep, bass hum, Elara spoke, her words measured.

“My father was a cartographer,” she said, not looking back. “He mapped the edges of the kingdom. The blank spaces. He believed the old maps were lies, not because they were wrong, but because they only showed what the crown wished to see. He taught me that a true map shows the obstacles. The treacherous ground. The places where the world resists.”

She fell silent as they crossed the ledge. On the other side, the tunnel began to slope upward. The air grew fresher, carrying the unmistakable tang of bog myrtle and stagnant water.

“He made a map of the fens,” she continued, her voice softer. “For the Witan. It showed the deep channels, the firm islands, the paths only the fen-folk knew. The king’s men came for it. He would not give it. He said it was not his to give; it belonged to the people of the mire. They called it treason. He died in a dungeon, his eyes put out with hot irons so he could never map again. I was twelve. I took the map. I have carried it since.”

She did not say it was why she fought. She did not say it was why she was a traitor in the eyes of the crown. She simply stated it, a fact as solid as the stone around them. It was her thread. Her connection.

Cillian understood then. They were all threads, frayed and stained, being woven back into a pattern that had been unraveling for a long, long time.

The darkness began to grey. The lantern’s flame grew pale and insignificant. A faint, greenish light seeped into the tunnel from ahead, and the sound of dripping water was joined by the croak of frogs and the whisper of reeds. The air was thick and humid.

The tunnel ended in a curtain of hanging roots and thick moss. Lothair extinguished the lantern. They pushed through the living veil into the late afternoon of another world.

The fens stretched before them, a vast, flat expanse of water and sky. Islands of dense, dark green clustered like the backs of sleeping beasts in a silver-grey sea of channels and meres. The sky was a sheet of hammered lead, low and heavy, bleeding a soft, directionless light onto the water. The silence here was different from the mountain’s—a vast, humming quiet, broken by the occasional plop of a fish or the distant, lonely cry of a waterbird. The air smelled of peat, of fermentation, of profound and patient decay.

It was beautiful, and it was utterly desolate.

They stood on a small, firm hummock of grass, the mouth of the tunnel hidden behind them in a tangle of willow. Before them, a narrow channel of black water led into the maze.

“The inmost cave,” Lothair murmured. “Not a single place. A thousand.”

Elara was already scanning the horizon, her eyes narrowed. “Athelstan is here. Or will be by morning. He’ll have gone to the Stone Dyke, the only garrison landward. They’ll have boats. They’ll know the main channels.”

“But not your father’s map,” Cillian said.

A ghost of a smile touched Elara’s lips. It was a hard, sharp thing. “No. Not his map.”

From a tube of oiled leather inside her tunic, she drew a scroll. She did not unroll it fully, but showed a corner. The ink was faded but precise, showing a labyrinth of lines, with symbols for firm ground, for deep pools, for quaking bog that could swallow a regiment. In the center, a cluster of islets was marked with a symbol like a crown of woven reeds.

“The Witan meets here. The Isle of Voices. A day’s journey, if we move without rest. If the mist holds.”

“The mist will hold,” Lothair said, looking at the sky. “It always holds here.”

They prepared in the last of the light. Elara used her seax to cut three long, straight poles of grey willow from the thicket. She handed one to each of them. “For testing the bottom. And for balance. Step only where I step. The green grass lies. The brown grass sometimes tells the truth. The water is your friend; the ground is your enemy.”

She fashioned a makeshift pack from her cloak, securing the map tube and their few remaining supplies. Lothair checked his seax, then took a small whetstone from his pouch and began, with slow, deliberate strokes, to run it along the blade. The sound was a soft, rhythmic shush-shush that seemed to argue with the silence of the fens.

Cillian watched. This was the respite. The moment of bonding and final preparation. But there was no warmth in it. It was a cold, clear-eyed acknowledgment of their shared fate. They were three threads, knotted together, about to be tested against the loom of the mire.

“Why do you do this?” Cillian asked Lothair finally. “You are not like her. You had a place. A rank. You could have turned me in at the waterfall. Bought your peace.”

Lothair did not stop sharpening the blade. Shush-shush. “I took an oath,” he said. “To a king. A true one. Not the pale thing that sits in the High Hall now. That oath did not die with the man. It lives in the line. In the idea.” He looked up, his eyes catching the grey light. “But oaths are not enough. I had a son. He was your age. The new king’s peace took him. A conscription to fight a war over a border that had stood for five hundred years. He died in the mud of a field that meant nothing. The scroll that told me said he died with honor. It was a lie. There was no honor in that mud. Only waste.”

Shush-shush. The final stroke.

“So I do this for an oath to a ghost,” he said, sheathing the seax with a definitive click. “And for a boy in different mud. And because I am tired of lies.”

The silence returned, deeper now. The light was fading from grey to charcoal. The first tendrils of evening mist were uncurling from the channels, white and silent as ghosts.

“It is time,” Elara said. She stepped off the hummock onto what looked like a submerged log. It did not sink. She held her pole out for balance, a silhouette against the vast, watery plain. “Follow. Exactly.”

They walked on the skin of the world. Elara moved with an uncanny grace, her feet finding purchase on clumps of sedge, on hidden ridges of stone, on the gnarled, submerged roots of ancient alders. Sometimes she stepped into black water up to her thigh, and they would follow, the cold a shock that stole the breath. The mist thickened, wrapping around them in damp, clinging veils. Sound became distorted. The splash of their own movement seemed to come from everywhere and nowhere.

Cillian’s world shrank to the shape of Elara’s back, the placement of her feet, the probing arc of her pole. The fens did not feel like a place. They felt like a condition. A state of being between solidity and dissolution. Time lost meaning again, measured only in the growing ache of his muscles and the deepening of the dark.

Once, Elara froze, her hand raised. They stood, statues in the mist, listening. Cillian heard it then—a faint, rhythmic dip-drag, dip-drag of a pole in water. Not theirs. Somewhere to the left. It faded, swallowed by the fog.

“Hunters,” Lothair breathed. “Already on the water.”

They changed course, Elara consulting her mental map with a frown of concentration. The mist turned the world into a grey dream. Islands loomed suddenly like sleeping leviathans and faded away just as quickly. Stars, when they pierced the haze, were blurred and watery.

After an eternity, Elara led them up onto an island larger than the rest, crowned with a grove of twisted, wind-sculpted pines. The ground was firm. “We are close,” she whispered. “The Isle of Voices is just through that channel. We wait for first light. The Witan will not receive anyone in the dark. It is law.”

They huddled in the lee of the pines, shrouded in mist. They could not risk a fire. They ate a little cold, soaked oatcake. The damp seeped into Cillian’s bones. He was shivering, a fine, constant tremor.

Lothair sat with his back against a pine, his eyes on the channel where the mist swirled thickest. Elara sat cross-legged, her father’s map resting on her knees, her fingers tracing the lines she could not see.

“What are the voices?” Cillian asked, his teeth chattering slightly. “On the isle.”

“The fen-folk,” Elara said. “The elders. The last who remember the old compact between the land and the crown. They speak for the water, the reeds, the fish, the peat. They are the Witan. A king who cannot gain their voice is no king of this land, only its occupier.”

“And if they give me their voice?” Cillian asked.

Lothair spoke from the dark. “Then you cease to be a boy, or a prize, or a thread. You become a claim. And a war begins. Not with hunting horns in a gorge, but with the slow, quiet drowning of armies in the mire, and the poisoning of wells in the heartland, and the burning of granaries in the night. A war the crown cannot win, but will fight to the last man to avoid losing.” He turned his head. The mist coiled around his profile. “That is the future you stand at the edge of. The past future. The one that was supposed to be, before the murder in the dark.”

Cillian fell silent. He looked out at the channel. The mist was moving, parting in a slow, deliberate spiral, as if stirred by a giant, unseen hand. As it thinned, he saw it.

The Isle of Voices.

It was a low, flat mound rising from the silver water, crowned not with trees, but with standing stones. They were short, moss-covered, hunched like old men in council. In the center, a larger stone, pale and gleaming faintly with a lichen of its own, stood like a throne. Around the island’s perimeter, moored to stakes, were dozens of low, shallow-draft boats—fen skiffs, made of woven reed and pitch.

And on the shore, between the boats, figures stood waiting.

They were not soldiers. They were men and women of all ages, dressed in clothes of rough wool and cured leather, stained with peat and water. They held fishing spears, eel baskets, long poles. They were utterly still, facing the channel, facing the three of them hidden in the mist on the pine island.

They had been expected.

A figure stepped forward from the group on the shore. An old woman, her hair a white cloud around a face like carved bog oak. She raised a hand, not in greeting, but in a command for stillness. Her voice, when it came, carried across the water with impossible clarity. It was the voice of the fen itself—deep, wet, and old.

“We have heard the hunt in our waters. We have felt the wrongness in the air. The ledger of the world is out of balance.” She paused, her eyes seeming to pierce the mist and find Cillian’s own. “The last thread of the old line stands in the mist. The past future knocks at our door. Step forward. Let the water see you. Let the stone hear your name.”

Elara and Lothair looked at Cillian. This was the threshold. The entrance to the greatest danger. Not of capture or death, but of meaning. Of becoming the claim.

He stood. His legs were weak, but he stood. He walked to the edge of their island, the mist curling away from him as he moved. He felt, rather than saw, Elara and Lothair rise to stand behind him, flanking him.

He looked across the black water at the assembly of silent, watching people. At the standing stones. At the old woman who was the voice of the world.

The path forward was clear. It was a few dozen paces of open, vulnerable water. It was an answer to a question he had not known how to ask.

He took a breath, tasting the air of the fens—the taste of peat, of patience, of endings and beginnings all tangled in the deep, slow rot of roots.

He stepped off the firm ground into the black, waiting water.

Chapter 6

CHAPTER SIX

The water was not cold. It was an absence of temperature, a liquid void that drank the heat from his bones and left in its place a profound, cellular stillness. It was the opposite of the river that had birthed him into this story. That river had been violent, full of direction. This water was patient. It did not flow; it waited. It had been waiting for centuries.

It closed over his head without a sound.

The world became a compression of dark green and deeper black. The silt, stirred by his entry, rose in slow, coiling clouds, like the breath of a submerged giant. He sank. His boots touched not mud, but something firm and ridged—a causeway of ancient logs, laid down in a time before memory, now preserved in the peat’s cold embrace. He was walking on a drowned road.

A hand closed around his upper arm. Lothair. The grip was iron, purposeful, and it pulled him forward through the gloom. He could see the big man’s shape ahead, a darker blot in the dark, moving with a surety that spoke of a map felt through the soles of the feet. Elara was a shadow to his left, her movements eel-smooth, her hair a pale weed drifting around her face. They were flanking him, as they had on the island, but here, in the swallowing dark, it felt less like protection and more like a solemn escort.

The submerged walkway sloped upward. His head broke the surface, and the air was a shock—cool, thick with the scent of wet stone and burning peat. He was standing in a channel no wider than a cart path, the water at his chest. Directly ahead, the bank was a wall of fitted stone, green with slick moss. A set of crude steps, carved into the living rock, led up to the old woman and the silent assembly.

They said nothing as he climbed, the water streaming from his clothes in rivers. He felt their eyes on him—not hostile, not welcoming, but assessing, as one might assess a strange fish pulled from the deep. The old woman stood at the top of the steps. Up close, her face was a landscape of fissures and hard angles, her eyes the colour of flint under water.

“The water has seen you,” she said, her voice low, for him alone. “It remembers the shape of you. Now, the stone.”

She turned. The crowd parted for her without a word, a human current flowing around a steadfast rock. They followed her through the village—a cluster of low, turf-roofed huts built on the only true hill in the endless fen. Canoes and reed-skiffs were drawn up on the muddy shores.
